Their report goes beyond the happy talk of "a world of connections" to specify ways in which digital platforms have become an increasingly toxic presence in everyday life:

Snapchat turns conversations into streaks, redefining how our children measure friendship.

Instagram glorifies the picture-perfect life, eroding our self-worth.

Facebook segregates us into echo chambers, fragmenting our communities.

YouTube autoplays the next video within seconds, even if it eats into our sleep.

These are not neutral products. They are part of a system designed to addict us.^[25](#part0022.html_id_14){#part0020.html_id_13 .calibre3}^

Using language that balances benefits and risks while carefully distributing responsibility for possible harms, the Center for Humane Technology offers the following analysis:

Phones, apps, and the web are so indispensable to our daily lives---a testament to the benefits they give us---that we've become a captive audience. With two billion people plugged into these devices, technology companies have inadvertently enabled a direct channel to manipulate entire societies with unprecedented precision. Technology platforms make it easier than ever for bad actors to cause havoc:

Pushing lies directly to specific zip codes, races, or religions.

Finding people who are already prone to conspiracies or racism, and automatically reaching similar users with "Lookalike" targeting.

Delivering messages timed to prey on us when we are most emotionally vulnerable (e.g., Facebook found depressed teens buy more makeup).

Creating millions of fake accounts and bots impersonating real people with real-sounding names and photos, fooling millions with the false impression of consensus.

Meanwhile, the platform companies profit from growth in users and activity.^[26](#part0022.html_id_12){#part0020.html_id_11 .calibre3}^

The use of such terms as "inadvertently" and "bad actors" signals that the center wants to avoid appearing critical of Silicon []{#part0020.html_page_194 .calibre1}Valley enterprises as a whole. Its underlying message is that the technologies are basically just fine, merely subject to occasional lapses, unintended consequences, and the schemes of evildoers. Even though its own analysis points to the fact that Facebook and other firms have deliberately engineered their products to be addictive, the center's web page faults them not for any malign intent but only for blindly seeking profit.

The proposals of the tech humanists emphasize a need for "humane design," a collection of technological fixes that selectively eliminate harmful features of digital technology. Thus, the Center for Humane Technology suggests that Apple, Samsung, and Microsoft "can redesign their devices and core interfaces to protect our minds from constant distractions, minimize screen time, protect our time in relationships. . . ."^[27](#part0022.html_id_10){#part0020.html_id_9 .calibre3}^ One project of this kind is Tristan Harris's Time Well Spent, a nonprofit organization that seeks to alter the software of Internet platforms so that their users are encouraged to go beyond the trivial habits that social media support and to draw upon more deeply resonant "values," especially the time they spend in meaningful relationships with other people. Warming to this challenge, in early 2018 Mark Zuckerberg, CEO of Facebook, took steps to appropriate the Time Well Spent project as an attractive option within Facebook's elaborate settings. From a commercial standpoint, however, the experiment proved problematic, since it led to a decrease of 50 million hours in users' time per day on Facebook, a potentially serious hit to the firm's advertising revenues.^[28](#part0022.html_id_8){#part0020.html_id_7 .calibre3}^

Remedies of this sort resonate with another Silicon Valley fashion of recent years---the sponsorship of "wellness" programs for corporate personnel. Often expressed in special sessions offering yoga and meditation during the workday or at special retreats, training in these skills is seen as a boost to one's personal health and an important contribution to a firm's capacity for innovation. It comes as no surprise that, once again, the basic purpose of the wellness industry is to discover and enhance the exquisite "Me" from which all else is believed to flow. The idea now is to go more deeply within oneself to find one's true ground of being.^[29](#part0022.html_id_6){#part0020.html_id_5 .calibre3}^

As a modest political expression of their quest, tech humanists advocate joining with others to "apply political pressure" on lawmakers to advance creative reforms. The Center for Humane Technology explains, "Governments can pressure tech[]{#part0020.html_page_195 .calibre1}nology companies toward humane business models by including the negative externalities of attention extraction on their balance sheets, and creating better protection for consumers."^[30](#part0022.html_id_4){#part0020.html_id_3 .calibre3}^ But protection of what kind? Presumably a better shielded autonomy for one's beloved sense of self.

A more useful step would be to seek forms of politically humane technology by exploring ways to build links between Internet communications and forums for face-to-face citizen debate, cooperation, and policy making. If, as Arendt argues, "the lost treasure of the revolution" involves small, local democratic groups able to affect larger national political outcomes, then the obvious challenge is to start building and invigorating these bodies again. Salvation of a now-fading democracy is readily at hand, I would argue, if only that path were to be chosen. The fact that most leaders of modern revolutions have consciously, deliberately rejected institutions of direct democracy is no reason to replicate their failings within today's public realms, ones that might (even now) connect online and face-to-face political life in the years to come.

Alas, even in proposed remedies for today's flagrant abuses of online power it is still the concerns of consumerism and techno-narcissism that are emphasized above all. From the perspective of Hannah Arendt there is as yet in today's discussions and practices of online "politics" little or no understanding that moving into the public realm entails relinquishing "the private self, the cherished self of our hopes and dreams" and becoming a person who makes himself or herself "known in word and deed." This means, in effect, that genuine political participation involves the advent of a second self, one whose character is ultimately determined by the perceptions and judgments of one's fellow citizens and of historical memory, not of oneself alone.

While possibilities of this kind may seem abstract and inaccessible, a closer look reveals some basic down-to-earth choices. In important ways the pathway to a lively citizenship begins with the simple choice to "show up!"---to attend public meetings, to join public groups, to march in demonstrations, to speak up in civic gatherings, and to become active in groups that seek to address and improve the institutions and practices of community life. In every city, town, or village of the nation, opportunities of this kind are readily available and are well worth exploring.^[31](#part0022.html_id_2){#part0020.html_id_1 .calibre3}^

[]{#part0020.html_page_196 .calibre1}Arendt believed that to enter the public realm is a potentially marvelous step. For it reveals a person in entirely new settings and in a surprising new light, offering fruitful courses of action for the community as a whole. And yes, of necessity, to engage in action in the public realm involves risk, including the risk of highly uncertain, perhaps even perilous, outcomes both for oneself and for the others one encounters. Fortunately, experiences of this kind may well generate something otherwise completely unattainable---genuine political freedom.

[]{#part0021.html}

[]{#part0021.html_page_197 .calibre3}AFTERWORD

IN JUNE 2016 the Pacific Gas and Electric Company (PG&E) announced that it would close its Diablo Canyon nuclear power plant in 2025. Although the firm had previously planned to extend the facility's operating license for another thirty years, news of the 2011 disaster in Fukushima, Japan---the massive earthquake, tsunami, and meltdowns of four nuclear reactors---finally swept away what little public support for atomic power remained in California at the time. Now planning has begun for decommissioning Diablo Canyon, a process that could last several decades at an estimated cost of \$4.8 billion.

Enthusiasm for domestic nuclear energy emerged during the early 1950s as an expression of widespread hope that "atoms for peace" might skillfully harness the destructive force of bombs like those dropped on Hiroshima and Nagasaki as well as even more powerful weapons produced during the Cold War. Many scientists, public officials, and concerned citizens sought ways to steer this marvelous new technology toward more humane purposes. In 1954 businessman Lewis Strauss, an early member of the Atomic Energy Commission, brashly predicted that atomic power would soon produce electricity "too cheap to meter." Visions of that kind inspired a generation of engineers, government bureaucrats, and utility company executives to begin building dozens of nuclear power stations around the nation.

An especially ambitious realization of the dream was an enormous facility with two pressurized water reactors built in []{#part0021.html_page_198 .calibre1}the hills of central coastal California, not far from the town of San Luis Obispo, just around the corner from the funky little sun-and-fun tourist haven of Avila Beach. As construction began in 1968, the plant was widely heralded as a marvel of American technological prowess and far-sighted business strategy. Careful to avoid visual blight along the beautiful shoreline, PG&E chose the chaparral-covered hills of Diablo Canyon as the plant's location, a site nicely shielded from the view of beachgoers as well as automobiles driving along Highway 101, yet close enough to the ocean to provide plenty of water to cool both reactor units.

Alas, the plant was ill-fated from the beginning. Built to withstand earthquakes of a size predictable for that section of the coast, the structure had to be thoroughly redesigned and rebuilt following the discovery of a previously unknown seismic fault several miles out to sea. The costly upgrade increased its ability to withstand earthquakes from those of 6.75 magnitude to those of 7.5 magnitude. But as the plant neared completion in the early 1980s, another calamity occurred: an inadvertent, long-unrecognized reversal of blueprints guided construction of the second reactor's support system in wonderfully precise but grossly inadequate dimensions until the technicians noticed the blunder and were forced to start over. Many years later, yet another threatening geologic menace, the Shoreline Fault, was discovered, prompting further inquiries into the plant's ultimate safety.

Despite (or perhaps because of) these recurring difficulties, during its heyday Diablo Canyon was renowned as perhaps the most scrupulously engineered and skillfully tended nuclear facility in the world. Missteps in its early construction required meticulous but expensive levels of engineering and technical management that inspired some observers to designate the plant "gold plated." In its maturity, proponents argued, Diablo Canyon would finally realize the safe, reliable, economically viable future for nuclear power that its early advocates envisioned.

As of summer 2018 the United States still had sixty commercially operational nuclear power plants with ninety-eight reactor units generating about 20 percent of the nation's electric energy. A renewed push during Barack Obama's administration to build a new generation of power plants succeeded []{#part0021.html_page_199 .calibre1}in promoting several new proposals for nuclear power plant construction, some of which actually broke ground. But faced with the colossal costs involved in financing their assembly, two new plants in South Carolina were eventually canceled, while two reactors under construction in Georgia were plagued by steadily rising mountains of debt.

To this day some observers argue that electricity from nuclear energy remains a viable, perhaps even urgently needed option for the long term as the world seeks to reduce its reliance on fossil fuels. Among the proponents of this path is James Hansen, a leading voice in the scientific community who warns about the dangers of climate change caused by the burning of coal, petroleum, and natural gas. Seldom noted in arguments of that kind, however, are the enormous quantities of fossil fuels expended during the full life span of nuclear power plants. Fossil fuels are used for the mining, transport, and refinement of uranium into usable fuel; the preparation of reactor sites; the energy-intensive manufacturing of the enormous pieces of equipment and protective housing needed in plants' construction; the preparations for "temporary" on-site storage of nuclear wastes; and the decades-long decommissioning of obsolete facilities, as well as the as yet unfinished tasks of building, operating, and protecting storage facilities for nuclear wastes in their centuries-long burial places. It turns out that nuclear energy has a substantial carbon footprint.

Taking into account energy returned on energy invested as well as the sheer dollar costs of energy solutions, electricity "too cheap to meter" now seems a mere pipe dream. The best prospects for nuclear technology's future lie in the dynamics of authoritarian political regimes, where calculations of total electricity output seem to justify the enormous economic, environmental, and social costs of nuclear construction and operation. At present, for example, China is building or preparing to build a dozen new plants, regarded as a much-needed contribution to the country's growing economy.

Beginning in the late 1970s, the Diablo Canyon plant became the focus of continuing opposition by local and national environmental groups, most notably by the Abalone Alliance organized in San Luis Obispo. Worries about background radiation, the ecological disruption of coastal wildlife from the dumping of warm water, the danger of nuclear accidents and []{#part0021.html_page_200 .calibre1}possible meltdown, the handling of on-site radioactive waste, and numerous other concerns led to recurring demonstrations and even occasional blockades of roads leading to the plant. Compounding PG&E's difficulties, another crisis has emerged as the result of widespread wildfires that now afflict California. As I write this, some thirty billion dollars in claims have been filed, citing the firm's equipment as the possible source of a series of conflagrations that have swept across the state, including a firestorm that completely destroyed the town of Paradise in a few hours.

In recent years, a number of organizations (including ones previously at loggerheads) have finally joined forces in an effort to close the Diablo Canyon plant once and for all. Working with environmental groups eager to build networks of wind and solar power, listening carefully to the expressed needs of labor unions and local political officials, fully aware of projections that point to diminishing demand for centrally generated, grid-distributed electricity in California, PG&E eventually relinquished any plans to give the plant a second run. Now the future of energy in the state depends on renewables. Thus, a colorful, heavily conflicted chapter in the history of technology arrives at a reasonably agreeable conclusion.

Langdon Winner

May 2019

CHAPTER 4. *Building the Better Mousetrap*

[1](#part0012.html_id_319){#part0022.html_id_320 .calibre1}. [6](#part0012.html_id_309){#part0022.html_id_310 .calibre1}. Perhaps the best expression of his tendency is to be found in Karl Hess, *Dear America* (New York: Morrow, 1975), chaps. 8--10 and Hess's *Community Technology* (New York: Harper & Row, 1979).

[]{#part0022.html_page_210 .calibre1}CHAPTER 7. *The State of Nature Revisited*

[1](#part0016.html_id_175){#part0022.html_id_176 .calibre1}. Ralph Waldo Emerson, "Nature," in *The Selected Writings of Ralph Waldo Emerson*, Brooks Atkinson (ed.) (New York: Modern Library, 1940).

[2](#part0016.html_id_173){#part0022.html_id_174 .calibre1}. General introductions to changing conceptions of nature in Western culture include Clarence J. Y. Crowell, 1973).

Kneese, *The Economics of Environmental Policy* (New York: John Wiley, 1973); William J. Baumol and Wallace E. Oates, *The Theory of Environmental Policy* (Englewood Cliffs, N.J.: Prentice-Hall, 1975); and Frederick Anderson et al., *Environmental Improvement Through Economic Incentives* (Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University Press, 1977).

Fisher, *The Economics of Natural Environments* (Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University Press, 1974). See also the general discussion in Joseph M. Petulla, *American Environmentalism: Values, Tactics and Priorities* (College Station: Texas A&M Press, 1980), chap. 5.

[13](#part0016.html_id_151){#part0022.html_id_152 .calibre1}. Typical of economic thinking about "trade-offs" in environmental policy is William F. Baxter, *People or Penguins* (New York: Columbia University Press, 1974). A lively critique of cost/benefit analysis as applied to environmental decision making is presented in Mark Sagoff, "Economic Theory and Environmental Law," *Michigan Law Review* 79: 1393--1419, 1981.

[17](#part0016.html_id_143){#part0022.html_id_144 .calibre1}. A poignant treatment of this theme is Jonathan Schell, *The Fate of the Earth* (New York: Alfred A. Knopf, 1982). "Nature, once a harsh and feared master, now lies in subjection, and needs protection against man's powers. Yet because man, no matter what intellectual and technical heights he may scale, remains embedded in nature, the balance has shifted against him too, and the threat he poses to the earth is a threat to him as well." (110)

CHAPTER 8. *Risk Assessment: A Hazardous Pursuit*

[1](#part0017.html_id_107){#part0022.html_id_108 .calibre1}. Samuel S. Epstein, Lester O. Brown, and Carl Pope, *Hazardous Waste in America* (San Francisco: Sierra Club Books, 1982), 6.

[2](#part0017.html_id_105){#part0022.html_id_106 .calibre1}. Stephen Salsbury, *The State, the Investor, and the Railroad: Boston & Albany, 1825--1867* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 1967), 182--187.

[3](#part0017.html_id_103){#part0022.html_id_104 .calibre1}. Upton Sinclair, *The Jungle* (New York: Doubleday, 1906). See also Leon A. Harris, *Upton Sinclair, American Rebel* (New York: Crowell, 1975).

[4](#part0017.html_id_101){#part0022.html_id_102 .calibre1}. Ivan Illich, *Medical Nemesis: The Expropriation of Health* (New York: Pantheon Books, 1976), 3. "Increasing and irreparable damage," Illich writes, "accompanies present industrial expansion in all sectors. In medicine this damage appears as iatrogenesis. Iatrogenesis is clinical when pain, sickness, and death result from medical care; it is social when health policies reinforce an industrial organization that generates ill-health." (270--271)

[5](#part0017.html_id_99){#part0022.html_id_100 .calibre1}. See, for example, Mark Green and Robert Massie, Jr. (eds.), *The Big Business Reader* (New York: Pilgrim Press, 1980), especially Edward Greer, "And Filthy Flows the Calumet," 169--179; Michael H. Brown, "Love Canal and the Poisoning of America," 189--207; and Mark Dowie, "The Dumping of Hazardous Products on Foreign Markets," 430--443.

[6](#part0017.html_id_97){#part0022.html_id_98 .calibre1}. Two important writings that helped place the concept of "risk" at the center of discussions about technology, environment, and society are Chauncey Starr, "Social Benefit Versus Technological Risk," *Science* 165:1232--[]{#part0022.html_page_213 .calibre1}1238, 1969; and William Lowrance, *Of Acceptable Risk* (Los Altos: William Kaufmann, 1976).

[7](#part0017.html_id_95){#part0022.html_id_96 .calibre1}. Steven D. Jellinek laments this state of affairs in "On the Inevitability of Being Wrong," *Technology Review* 82(8):8--9, 1980.

[8](#part0017.html_id_93){#part0022.html_id_94 .calibre1}. Dante Picciano, "Pilot Cytogenetic Study of Love Canal, New York," prepared by the Biogenics Corporation for the Environmental Protection Agency, May 14, 1980; quoted in *Hazardous Waste in America*, 113--114.

[9](#part0017.html_id_91){#part0022.html_id_92 .calibre1}. See Edmund A. C. Crouch and Richard Wilson, *Risk/Benefit Analysis* (Cambridge: Ballinger, 1982).

[10](#part0017.html_id_89){#part0022.html_id_90 .calibre1}. See Baruch Fischoff et al., *Acceptable Risk* (Cambridge: Cambridge Unviersity Press, 1981).

[11](#part0017.html_id_87){#part0022.html_id_88 .calibre1}. Chauncey Starr, Richard Rudman, and Chris Whipple, "Philosophical Basis for Risk Analysis," *Annual Review of Energy* 1:629--662, 1976.

[12](#part0017.html_id_85){#part0022.html_id_86 .calibre1}. Samuel C. Florman, "Technophobia in Modern Times," *Science '82*, 3:14, 1982.

[13](#part0017.html_id_83){#part0022.html_id_84 .calibre1}. See my review, "Pollution as Delusion," *New York Times Book Review*, August 8, 1982, 8, 18.

[14](#part0017.html_id_81){#part0022.html_id_82 .calibre1}. From "Observations," an advertisement of the Mobil Oil Corporation, *Parade Magazine*, December 12, 1982, 29.

Among the helpful criticisms of cost/benefit analysis are Mark Sagoff, "Economic Theory and Environmental Law," *Michigan Law Review*, 79:1393--1419, 1981; and Steve H. Hanke, "On the Feasibility of Benefit-Cost Analysis," *Public Policy* 29:147--157, 1981.

In *The Age of Missing Information* McKibben describes his experience, noting that he had not previously spent much time watching television at all. This is not surprising, because he had long since fashioned a life of scholarly, []{#part0022.html_page_215 .calibre1}philosophical, and political engagement with questions that have to do with humanity's relationship to nature and the need to preserve natural habitats and species now threatened by the hazards of modern industrial development. In fact, by the time he embarked on his cable TV project, McKibben had already won fame as the first nonscientist to rally public attention to the threat posed by global warming and climate change. Thus, with more or less pristine eyes, he took note of the features and thematic patterns he observed.
